
=== persist-001 ===
Q: How do I add persistence to a LangGraph agent?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To add persistence to a LangGraph  │
│ agent, you can use LangGraph's     │
│ persistence features. This         │
│ involves storing message history   │
│ in a storage backend such as       │
│ SQLite, Postgres, or Azure Cosmos  │
│ DB, and then fetching previous     │
│ messages from state when routing   │
│ to an agent.                       │
│                                    │
│ Here are the steps:                │
│                                    │
│  1 Implement memory in LangGraph   │
│    for AI applications by          │
│    following the guide             │
│    Implementing Memory in          │
│    LangGraph for AI Applications.  │
│  2 Extend AgentState to add        │
│    additional fields using a       │
│    custom state schema.            │
│  3 Pass the custom state schema to │
│    create_agent using the          │
│    state_schema parameter.         │
│  4 Use persistence to store        │
│    message history in a storage    │
│    backend.                        │
│                                    │
│ For example:                       │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  conversational_agent =            │
│  create_agent(                     │
│      model,                        │
│      tools=[search_docs],          │
│      prompt="You are a helpful     │
│  assistant. Use search_docs to     │
│  answer questions.",               │
│      state_schema={"messages":     │
│  {"type": "list", "items":         │
│  {"type": "dict"}}}                │
│  )                                 │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ This will add persistence to the   │
│ agent, allowing it to maintain     │
│ state and continue conversations   │
│ using the same thread ID.          │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== concepts-001 ===
Q: What's the difference between StateGraph and MessageGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ Based on the context from          │
│ LangChain documentation and web    │
│ results, here are the differences  │
│ between StateGraph and             │
│ MessageGraph:                      │
│                                    │
│ StateGraph:                        │
│                                    │
│  • A smart flowchart that carries  │
│    and updates a bag of info       │
│    (state) at every step.          │
│  • It merges the state for you     │
│    from node to node, and enforces │
│    types.                          │
│                                    │
│ MessageGraph:                      │
│                                    │
│  • Does not have these features as │
│    StateGraph does.                │
│  • Used in LangGraph for managing  │
│    and evolving multi-turn message │
│    history with minimal            │
│    boilerplate.                    │
│                                    │
│ In summary, StateGraph is a more   │
│ advanced feature that carries and  │
│ updates state at every step, while │
│ MessageGraph is a simpler          │
│ component used to manage message   │
│ history.                           │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

Q: Show me how to implement human-in-the-loop with LangGraph

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To implement human-in-the-loop     │
│ (HIL) with LangGraph, you can      │
│ follow these steps:                │
│                                    │
│  1 Understand the interrupt()      │
│    function: The interrupt()       │
│    function is used to pause       │
│    execution of a graph for human  │
│    input. It takes two arguments:  │
│    the first is the index at which │
│    to interrupt, and the second is │
│    the prompt that will be         │
│    displayed to the user.          │
│    Example:                        │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ from langgraph import Graph        │
│                                    │
│         Create a new graph         │
│                                    │
│ g = Graph()                        │
│                                    │
│  Add some nodes and edges to the   │
│               graph                │
│                                    │
│ g.add_node("node1")                │
│ g.add_edge("node1", "node2")       │
│                                    │
│   Interrupt execution at index 0   │
│           with a prompt            │
│                                    │
│ interrupt(0, "Please enter your    │
│ response:")                        │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  2. **Create a wrapper to add HIL  │
│  capabilities**: To seamlessly     │
│  integrate HIL features into any   │
│  tool, you can create a wrapper    │
│  around the `interrupt()`          │
│  function.                         │
│                                    │
│     Example:                       │
│     ```python                      │
│  from langgraph import Graph       │
│                                    │
│  class HumanInTheLoopWrapper:      │
│      def __init__(self, graph):    │
│          self.graph = graph        │
│                                    │
│      def run(self):                │
│          # Run the graph as        │
│  normal                            │
│          self.graph.run()          │
│                                    │
│          # Interrupt execution at  │
│  index 0 with a prompt             │
│          interrupt(0, "Please      │
│  enter your response:")            │
│                                    │
│  # Create a new graph and wrapper  │
│  g = Graph()                       │
│  wrapper =                         │
│  HumanInTheLoopWrapper(g)          │
│                                    │
│  # Run the wrapped graph           │
│  wrapper.run()                     │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  3 Use the interrupt() function in │
│    workflows: The interrupt()      │
│    function can be used to pause   │
│    execution of a graph for human  │
│    input in various workflows.     │
│    Example:                        │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ from langgraph import Graph        │
│                                    │
│         Create a new graph         │
│                                    │
│ g = Graph()                        │
│                                    │
│  Add some nodes and edges to the   │
│               graph                │
│                                    │
│ g.add_node("node1")                │
│ g.add_edge("node1", "node2")       │
│                                    │
│  Define a workflow that uses the   │
│        interrupt() function        │
│                                    │
│ def workflow(): # Run the graph as │
│ normal g.run()                     │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  # Interrupt execution at index 0  │
│  with a prompt                     │
│  interrupt(0, "Please enter your   │
│  response:")                       │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│          Run the workflow          │
│                                    │
│ workflow()                         │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  4. **Integrate HIL controls into  │
│  chatbots**: To integrate HIL      │
│  controls into chatbots, you can   │
│  use the `interrupt()` function    │
│  to pause execution of a graph     │
│  for human input.                  │
│                                    │
│     Example:                       │
│     ```python                      │
│  from langgraph import Graph       │
│                                    │
│  class Chatbot:                    │
│      def __init__(self):           │
│          self.graph = Graph()      │
│                                    │
│      def run(self):                │
│          # Run the graph as        │
│  normal                            │
│          self.graph.run()          │
│                                    │
│          # Interrupt execution at  │
│  index 0 with a prompt             │
│          interrupt(0, "Please      │
│  enter your response:")            │
│                                    │
│  # Create a new chatbot and run    │
│  it                                │
│  chatbot = Chatbot()               │
│  chatbot.run()                     │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  5 Use breakpoints to pause        │
│    execution: To use breakpoints   │
│    in LangGraph, you can set       │
│    breakpoints at compile time or  │
│    runtime using the breakpoint()  │
│    function.                       │
│    Example:                        │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ from langgraph import Graph        │
│                                    │
│         Create a new graph         │
│                                    │
│ g = Graph()                        │
│                                    │
│  Add some nodes and edges to the   │
│               graph                │
│                                    │
│ g.add_node("node1")                │
│ g.add_edge("node1", "node2")       │
│                                    │
│    Set a breakpoint at index 0     │
│                                    │
│ breakpoint(0)                      │
│                                    │
│      Run the graph as normal       │
│                                    │
│ g.run()                            │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  6. **Resuming execution after     │
│  hitting a breakpoint**: To        │
│  resume execution after hitting a  │
│  breakpoint, you can use the       │
│  `continue()` function.            │
│                                    │
│     Example:                       │
│     ```python                      │
│  from langgraph import Graph       │
│                                    │
│  # Create a new graph              │
│  g = Graph()                       │
│                                    │
│  # Add some nodes and edges to     │
│  the graph                         │
│  g.add_node("node1")               │
│  g.add_edge("node1", "node2")      │
│                                    │
│  # Set a breakpoint at index 0     │
│  breakpoint(0)                     │
│                                    │
│  # Resume execution after hitting  │
│  the breakpoint                    │
│  continue()                        │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ By following these steps, you can  │
│ implement human-in-the-loop (HIL)  │
│ features in LangGraph and          │
│ seamlessly integrate them into     │
│ various workflows.                 │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

Q: How do I handle errors and retries in LangGraph nodes?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To handle errors and retries in    │
│ LangGraph nodes, you can use one   │
│ of two approaches:                 │
│                                    │
│  1 Write regular Python code       │
│    within your node to catch and   │
│    handle exceptions.              │
│  2 Set a retry_policy to direct    │
│    the graph to retry nodes that   │
│    raise certain types of          │
│    exceptions.                     │
│                                    │
│ Additionally, you can control the  │
│ maximum number of concurrent tasks │
│ by setting max_concurrency in the  │
│ configuration when invoking the    │
│ graph.                             │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

Q: What are best practices for state management in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ Based on the context and web       │
│ results provided, the best         │
│ practices for state management in  │
│ LangGraph include:                 │
│                                    │
│  1 Defining the state clearly:     │
│    This involves understanding     │
│    what information needs to be    │
│    stored and how it will be used  │
│    throughout the workflow.        │
│  2 Controlling how updates merge:  │
│    This is achieved through the    │
│    use of reducers in LangGraph,   │
│    which determine how updates are │
│    combined when multiple agents   │
│    update the same state.          │
│  3 Persisting the state: This can  │
│    be done using checkpointers     │
│    such as SQLite, Redis, or       │
│    Postgres to ensure that the     │
│    state is saved and can be       │
│    recovered in case of failures.  │
│  4 Mastering state schema,         │
│    reducers, persistence, and      │
│    concurrency handling: These are │
│    key components of building      │
│    resilient, auditable, and       │
│    production-grade LangGraph      │
│    workflows.                      │
│                                    │
│ Additionally, web results suggest  │
│ that LangGraph provides features   │
│ such as:                           │
│                                    │
│  • Extended fields for context     │
│    tracking and memory control     │
│  • Memory window control for       │
│    efficient processing            │
│  • Attributes such as messages,    │
│    summary, and window_size to     │
│    manage conversation context and │
│    history                         │
│                                    │
│ It is also recommended to use      │
│ LangGraph's built-in features such │
│ as Secret Manager for              │
│ configuration and sensitive data,  │
│ Cloud Logging for monitoring, and  │
│ Artifact Registry for deploying    │
│ Agent Server images.               │
│                                    │
│ Overall, the best practices for    │
│ state management in LangGraph      │
│ involve a combination of clear     │
│ definition, controlled updates,    │
│ persistence, and mastery of key    │
│ components.                        │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== tools-001 ===
Q: How do I add tools to a LangGraph agent?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To add tools to a LangGraph agent, │
│ follow these steps:                │
│                                    │
│  1 In the agent editor, scroll to  │
│    the Tools section.              │
│  2 Click + Add tool.               │
│  3 Search for "Slack" and add the  │
│    tools you need:                 │
│     • slack_send_channel_message - │
│       Post messages to a channel   │
│     • slack_reply_to_message -     │
│       Reply in a thread            │
│     • slack_write_private_message  │
│       - Send direct messages       │
│     • slack_read_channel_history - │
│       Read recent messages         │
│     • slack_read_thread_messages - │
│       Read thread replies          │
│                                    │
│ Alternatively, you can also ask    │
│ your agent to add these tools      │
│ itself by typing "Add the Slack    │
│ tools so you can respond to        │
│ messages" in the agent chat.       │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== subgraph-001 ===
Q: How do I create and use subgraphs in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To create and use subgraphs in     │
│ LangGraph, you need to define how  │
│ the parent graph and the subgraph  │
│ communicate. There are two ways to │
│ do this:                           │
│                                    │
│  1 Shared state schemas: In this   │
│    case, the parent and subgraph   │
│    have shared state keys in their │
│    state schemas.                  │
│  2 Different state schemas: In     │
│    this case, there are no shared  │
│    state keys between the parent   │
│    and subgraph schemas.           │
│                                    │
│ To set up LangGraph for            │
│ development, you can install it    │
│ using pip:                         │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  pip install -U langgraph          │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ Additionally, you can sign up for  │
│ LangSmith to quickly spot issues   │
│ and improve the performance of     │
│ your LangGraph projects.           │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== memory-001 ===
Q: What's the difference between short-term and long-term memory in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ According to the provided context, │
│ the main differences between       │
│ short-term and long-term memory in │
│ LangGraph are:                     │
│                                    │
│  1 Scope:                          │
│     • Short-term memory is         │
│       thread-scoped, meaning it is │
│       saved within a single        │
│       thread.                      │
│     • Long-term memory is saved    │
│       within custom "namespaces"   │
│       and is shared across         │
│       conversational threads.      │
│  2 Purpose:                        │
│     • Short-term memory is used to │
│       remember previous            │
│       interactions within a single │
│       conversation or session.     │
│     • Long-term memory stores      │
│       user-specific or             │
│       application-level data       │
│       across sessions and is       │
│       shared across conversational │
│       threads, allowing it to be   │
│       recalled at any time and in  │
│       any thread.                  │
│  3 Persistence:                    │
│     • Short-term memory is managed │
│       as part of the agent's       │
│       state, persisted via         │
│       thread-scoped checkpoints.   │
│     • Long-term memory uses stores │
│       (as mentioned in the         │
│       reference doc) to save and   │
│       recall long-term memories.   │
│  4 Accessibility:                  │
│     • Short-term memory is         │
│       accessible within a single   │
│       conversation or session,     │
│       while long-term memory can   │
│       be accessed across different │
│       conversations and sessions.  │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== conditional-001 ===
Q: How do I add conditional edges in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ You can add conditional edges in   │
│ LangGraph by using the             │
│ add_conditional_edges method,      │
│ which takes three arguments:       │
│                                    │
│  1 The name of the node that will  │
│    serve as the starting point for │
│    the conditional edge.           │
│  2 A function that returns a list  │
│    of edges to be added            │
│    conditionally.                  │
│  3 A dictionary where the keys are │
│    boolean values and the values   │
│    are the names of the nodes to   │
│    which the edges should be       │
│    routed.                         │
│                                    │
│ For example:                       │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  graph.add_conditional_edges("nod  │
│  e_a", continue_to_jokes, {True:   │
│  "node_b", False: "node_c"})       │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ This will add a conditional edge   │
│ from node_a that routes to either  │
│ node_b or node_c depending on the  │
│ value of the boolean expression.   │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== parallel-001 ===
Q: Can LangGraph run nodes in parallel? How?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ Yes, LangGraph can run nodes in    │
│ parallel through fan-out and       │
│ fan-in mechanisms, utilizing both  │
│ standard edges and                 │
│ conditional_edges. This allows for │
│ the execution of multiple          │
│ destination nodes concurrently as  │
│ part of the next superstep.        │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯
